    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<span id="freeTextContainer7277518021202207387">Ange en quête d'absolu ? Monstre sanguinaire ? Avant la guerre, Albert Camus conçoit Caligula, ainsi que Sisyphe ou Meursault (L'Étranger), comme un héros de l'Absurde. En 1945, la pièce est reçue comme une fable sur les horreurs du nazisme. Ses versions et ses mises en scène successives, l'évolution de la sensibilité du public ont contribué à faire de Caligula une des fig</span>
  <span id="freeText7277518021202207387" style="display:none">Ange en quête d'absolu ? Monstre sanguinaire ? Avant la guerre, Albert Camus conçoit Caligula, ainsi que Sisyphe ou Meursault (L'Étranger), comme un héros de l'Absurde. En 1945, la pièce est reçue comme une fable sur les horreurs du nazisme. Ses versions et ses mises en scène successives, l'évolution de la sensibilité du public ont contribué à faire de Caligula une des figures les plus troublantes de notre théâtre. À l'image du tyran se superposent, dans notre mémoire, les visages de Gérard Philipe, qui créa le rôle, et celui d'Albert Camus, qui mêla toujours au besoin de tendresse et à l'exigence de pureté une étrange «fixation au meurtre» et «cette violence intérieure» (Jean Grenier) qui anime son empereur romain.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7277518021202207387" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer6088274945875669133">Caligula : Emperor of Rome, Albert Camus<br/><br/>Caligula is a play written by Albert Camus, begun in 1938, and published for the first time in May 1944. The play was later the subject of numerous revisions. It was part of what the author called the "Cycle of the Absurd", with the novel The Stranger (1942) and the essay The Myth of Sisyphus (1942). <br/><br/>The play shows Caligula, Roman Emperor, torn by the death of Drusilla, his sister and lover. In Camus' version of events, Caligula eventually deliberately man</span>
  <span id="freeText6088274945875669133" style="display:none">Caligula : Emperor of Rome, Albert Camus<br /><br />Caligula is a play written by Albert Camus, begun in 1938, and published for the first time in May 1944. The play was later the subject of numerous revisions. It was part of what the author called the "Cycle of the Absurd", with the novel The Stranger (1942) and the essay The Myth of Sisyphus (1942). <br /><br />The play shows Caligula, Roman Emperor, torn by the death of Drusilla, his sister and lover. In Camus' version of events, Caligula eventually deliberately manipulates his own assassination. (Historically, this event took place January 24, AD 41.)<br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: یکی از روزهای ماه ژانویه سال 1975 میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: کالیگولا: نمایشنامه در چهار پرده؛ نویسنده: آلبر کامو؛ مترجم جمشید خسرو؛ تهران، کانون شهریار، 1329؛ در 72ص؛ موضوع نمایشنامه های نویسندگان فرانسوی - سده 20م<br /><br />عنوان: کالیگولا : نمایشنامه در چهار پرده؛ نویسنده: آلبر کامو؛ مترجم: ابوالحسن نجفی؛ تهران، نیل، 1346؛ در 129ص؛ چاپ دوم تهران، نیل، 1350؛ در 129ص؛ چاپ سوم تهران، کتاب زمان، 1357، در 155ص؛ چاپ دیگر تهران، کتاب زمان؛ 1382؛ در 151ص؛ شابک 9646380298؛ چاپ پنجم 1386؛ ششم 1388؛ <br /><br />عنوان: کالیگولا: نمایشنامه در چهار پرده؛ نویسنده: آلبر کامو؛ مترجم: شورانگیز فرخ؛ تهران، مروارید، خانه کتاب، 1347، در 142ص؛ چاپ دوم 1357؛ در 142 ص؛ چاپ دیگر: تهران، فیروزه، 1375؛ در 142ص؛ شابک: 9649048545؛ <br /><br />کالیگولا، یا همان «گایوس سزار، سال 12میلادی تا سال 41میلادی»، سومین امپراتور روم بودند، که از سال 37میلادی، تا سال 41میلادی، فرمان میراندند؛ نام مستعار «کالیگولا (پوتینهای کوچک)» را، سربازان ارتش پدرش، به او دادند، چرا که پوتین‌های سربازی کوچکی را، به پا میکرد؛ پس از آنکه «کالیگولا» امپراتور شد، سلامتی عقل خود را از دست داد؛ در یکی از وقایعی که توسط تاریخ نگار رومی «سیوتاتیوس (سال 69میلادی تا 104میلادی)» ثبت شده، «کالیگولا» به ارتش بسیار آموزش دیده و قدرتمند خود، دستور داد، در ساحل دریا، صدف حلزون گردآوری کنند؛ او به اسب مورد علاقه ی خود، لقب «کنسول (عنوان یکی از مسئولین رده بالای ارتش روم)» را داده بود؛ او با خواهر خود «دروسیلا» ازدواج کرد، اما بعدها ایشان را نیز کشتند؛ اعضای گارد محافظش «پراتورها»، در سال 41میلادی، او را به قتل رساندند<br /><br />تاریخ بهنگام رسانی 12/06/1399هجری خورشیدی؛ ا. شربیانی</span>
  <a data-text-id="6088274945875669133" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2180443938" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2180443938" />
    <a title="Amalia Gkavea" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/56789747-amalia-gkavea"><img alt="Amalia Gkavea" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1559158764p2/56789747.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2180443938?book_show_action=true">Nov 10, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amalia Gkavea" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amalia Gkavea" href="/user/show/56789747-amalia-gkavea">Amalia Gkavea</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=tragedies">tragedies</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=european-theatre">european-theatre</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=france">france</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=theatre">theatre</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2180443938" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6103800848611215235"><i>
  <b>‘’A man can't live without some reason for living.” </b>
</i><br/><br/>It is often the case when I find no particular connection with the novels of highly acclaimed writers but their plays resonate with me and become a point of reference in my collection. Such bright examples are Tolstoy, Gorky, Sartre and Camus. <br/>Each one of them has produced some of the most fascinating, world-changing literary works, yet it is their plays that placed them in my heart. When I first read ‘’The Stranger’’, there was very little</span>
  <span id="freeText6103800848611215235" style="display:none"><i>
  <b>‘’A man can't live without some reason for living.” </b>
</i><br /><br />It is often the case when I find no particular connection with the novels of highly acclaimed writers but their plays resonate with me and become a point of reference in my collection. Such bright examples are Tolstoy, Gorky, Sartre and Camus. <br />Each one of them has produced some of the most fascinating, world-changing literary works, yet it is their plays that placed them in my heart. When I first read ‘’The Stranger’’, there was very little that surprised me or touched me. Yet, ‘’The Possessed’’ and ‘’Caligula’’ have stayed with me ever since. Watching a recent outstanding Greek production in a beautiful theatre house in Athens, I felt -yet, again- the impact, the sheer joy of the unfolding, living theatre, of the grandeur that only plays can bring to the audience’s hearts. And I was happy to see that there are still satisfying productions being made in the land that gave birth to Theatre but lost its cultural identity through cheap, poorly-made TV...Anyway...<br /><br />Caligula is one of the most fascinating, infamous and intriguing figures of Roman History. Competing with Nero for the place of the King of Mad Emperors in the mind of the laymen, he is reputed for his cruelty, barbarism, sexual perversion and unlimited resources of finding new ways to entertain himself through violence and whatnot. As with Nero, the majority of recent historians have disputed the credibility of the sources, but this isn’t what concerns us here. Theirs is the attempt to verify what cannot be verified, unless we finally invent the time -travel machine and take a trip to good, old, glorious Rome (and count me in, because I love perverted Roman Emperors and in any case, everyone is better than the politicians that are currently holding the fates of all nations in their filthy hands…) But I digress….<br /><br />Camus’ Caligula can hardly be seen as a ruthless monster. We’re not in Gore Vidal’s territory here, thankfully. Caligula loses the one person he loved most, his sister, and he falls to pieces. Drusilla isn’t with him anymore, therefore the world may rot for all he cares. And perhaps, he’ll feel better if he assists in the speedy procession of this ‘’rotting’’. He feels nothing, desires nothing but absolute control and even this is questionable. He establishes a tyranny of frightening proportions and wants the moon, the impossible, because nothing matters to him in the end. He shines as one of the most memorable protagonists in the context of a historical play to ever grace the stages worldwide. He has this distorted notion of freedom -but is it as distorted as we think, or there are many fragments of truth in his views about what being a ‘’free man’’ really means?- of being alive and in control and yearns for everything he cannot have. <br /><br />Camus’s writing is modern, contemporary to the time of the play, but never alienated from the context of the character. The words are flowing, the action aims right into the heart and the mind of the reader and the watcher with ruthless precision. The scenes lead to the bitter (?) end. Caligula in the hands of Camus becomes a shuttered, broken man who believes in nothing and desires the impossible. He gives in to his pain and projects his agony to the people around him. But the people around him are his subjects and they make for the most direct and at the same time, dull plaything. What could be more human than that? When we are in deep pain and desperation, the world ceases to exist and the others become scapegoats, easy targets for our rage and wrath. We all have done it in our lives and we are certain to do it again. <br /><br />Camus creates a figure that couldn’t be a more realistic depiction of the darkest recesses of the human nature. Never mind the myths about the horse that became a Senator, or the complex head-cutting machine or the intercourses with brides and grooms alike. These have nothing to do with the heart of Camus’ play. These belong to ridiculous films for those who desire shock for shock value. It is a sacrilege to even discuss them in the same context with Theatre. In this glorious moment of the finest art the human beings ever created, Caligula holds a mirror that shows the true face of mankind in despair….<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6103800848611215235" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_755480986" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/755480986" />
    <a title="Rakhi Dalal" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3655942-rakhi-dalal"><img alt="Rakhi Dalal" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1557596257p2/3655942.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/755480986?book_show_action=true">Nov 03, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rakhi Dalal" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rakhi Dalal" href="/user/show/3655942-rakhi-dalal">Rakhi Dalal</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Those interested in absurdism</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Rakhi by: </span>
      Jon Quinton
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3655942-rakhi-dalal?shelf=albert-camus">albert-camus</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3655942-rakhi-dalal?shelf=nobel-prized-author">nobel-prized-author</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3655942-rakhi-dalal?shelf=absurdism">absurdism</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer755480986" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3505720476140238323">This play by Camus on the concept of absurd is based upon the <i>Lives of the Twelve Caesars</i> by the Latin historian, Suetonius. According to the work, Caius Caesar Caligula, third of the twelve Caesars, who came to power in 37AD at the age of twenty-five, ruled for four years until he was assassinated in 41AD by his Patricians.*<br/><br/>The reason of his assassination was his cruelty against everyone, which led him to murder people on whims. There are different accounts on his changed state of mind after th</span>
  <span id="freeText3505720476140238323" style="display:none">This play by Camus on the concept of absurd is based upon the <i>Lives of the Twelve Caesars</i> by the Latin historian, Suetonius. According to the work, Caius Caesar Caligula, third of the twelve Caesars, who came to power in 37AD at the age of twenty-five, ruled for four years until he was assassinated in 41AD by his Patricians.*<br /><br />The reason of his assassination was his cruelty against everyone, which led him to murder people on whims. There are different accounts on his changed state of mind after the death of his sister Drusilla, whom he intended to marry. But Camus chose to depict the state of absurd through the madness of Caesar. So he came up with this play which was written during the late 1930’s and was published around the time he published <i>The Stranger</i> and <i>The Myth of Sisyphus</i>. <br /><br />It is quite intriguing to observe the wide canvas of Camus’ thoughts in which he painted the absurd in different motifs. <b>A clerk in The Stranger, An Emperor in Caligula, A doctor in The Plague or A lawyer in The Fall</b>. All of them expressing absurd in different situations owing to different circumstances, the magnitude of their suffering remaining equal even while their inferences from their situations varied; indifference for Meursault, Nihilism for Caligula or Solidarity for Dr. Rieux. In <i>Sisyphus</i> Camus says, “A sub-clerk in the post office is equal to a Conqueror if consciousness is common to them.” Camus experimented with different states of mind while striving to reach for a finality which could make sense even in the awareness of absurd. <br /><br />Since God didn’t matter to him as he wasn’t born in a family of religiously adhered people; his only tryst with religion being his communion at the age of ten which was done more to confirm to a societal way than for religious purpose, he also couldn’t relate to the ideas proposed by either Theist or Atheist Existential philosophers. So he was trying to make a tabula rasa on the basis of which he could construct something. In this endeavour he experimented with absurd in different scenarios. His later works concentrated more on the affirmation of need of Solidarity to make some sense but I still think he could have come up with something more substantial if it wasn’t for his untimely death. <br /><br />Meursault’s absurd was reconciled with “the benign indifference of the Universe”, Dr. Rieux’s absurd with the feeling of solidarity for fellow sufferers whereas Caligula’s absurd was only reconciled with suicide (he kind of helped his patricians to murder him). <br /><br />In this play, Caligula becomes aware of the absurd when he realizes that “men are dying and they are not happy”. In order to overcome this feeling of despair, he tries to indulge in the extremities to the point of nihilism, so that he can at least experience his control over life. <br /><br /><i>CALIGULA: But I must know where to place it. And what is a firm hand to me, of what use to me is so much astonishing power if I can’t change the order of things? <b>If I can’t make the sun lie down in the east, until suffering decreases and lives are no longer expiring?</b> No, Caesonia, it is insignifi¬cant to sleep or to stay awake if I don’t have any influence over the direction of this world.</i><br /><br />In his awareness, Caligula yearns for the impossible, he wants moon. Since he cannot have the moon then everything else stands on equal footing. He yields to nihilism and becomes destructive. Though even this he experiences with the knowledge of futility. And perhaps that is why he aids Cherea in his own murder at the hands of his Patricians. In <i>Sisyphus</i> Camus says that Absurd doesn’t dictate death i.e. even if a man becomes aware of the absurd, suicide cannot be/ shouldn’t be the outcome of that because it will undermine the value of life. But in Caligula, he further rejects the nihilism of the emperor which threatens the human existence, making it devoid of a meaning, so that his murder, which also seems like a suicide, is the act of restoring a greater truth proposed by Camus i.e. though the Universe be absurd but the absurd man cannot disown his rebellion by yielding to destruction thereby threatening the human life. <br /><br /><i>FIRST PATRICIAN: He wants our death before anything.<br />CHEREA: No, that is secondary. Now he employs his power in the service of a higher and more deadly passion. He threatens us through what we hold most deeply. Without doubt, this isn’t the first time that, among us, a man has com¬mand of an unlimited power. But it’s the first time that he uses it without constraints; as far as to deny the existence of mankind and the world. That is the thing which alarms me about him and what I wish to oppose. To lose one’s life is no great matter and I’ll have this courage when it will be necessary to do so. <b>But to see the meaning of this life dissipated, our reason to exist disappear, that’s the thing which is unbearable. A man cannot live without reason.</b></i><br /><br />Caligula is an important play, adding another perspective to the oeuvre of Camus’ works on absurd. <br /><br /> <br />-----------------------------------------------------------------------<br /><br /><br />*From the Notes on Caligula by Christopher Williams.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3505720476140238323" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer7783407658086838484">The Entire review is pretty much a spoiler, in some aspect, for the play by Albert Camus, Caligula.<br/><br/><b> "Men die; and they're not happy." </b><br/><br/>Death makes a mockery of everything we adhere values and virtues close to all our lives. Camus depicts an anomaly via Caligula Caius Caesar, one of the Roman Emperors known for his eccentric administration, if not for his cruelty and killing of his own people. He hadn't cared to maintain a proper system as it doesn't mean anything. His will to do whatever he wante</span>
  <span id="freeText7783407658086838484" style="display:none">The Entire review is pretty much a spoiler, in some aspect, for the play by Albert Camus, Caligula.<br><br><b> "Men die; and they're not happy." </b><br><br>Death makes a mockery of everything we adhere values and virtues close to all our lives. Camus depicts an anomaly via Caligula Caius Caesar, one of the Roman Emperors known for his eccentric administration, if not for his cruelty and killing of his own people. He hadn't cared to maintain a proper system as it doesn't mean anything. His will to do whatever he wanted, the impossible in his words, tend to give him the absurd value that he held to for his life to move on by subjugating and instilling fear upon his patricians and plebeians. Nero, the last one of the first Roman imperial dynasty, who was often in times referred to as "the Antichrist" for his cruelty towards people especially Christians, was born around the this time. There were very few emperors depicted as mad kings down in the history and We would never see its light except for pondering the possibilities of how they really lived and felt things.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1532521579i/26026224._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>As portrayed in the play, he had been never much alive in his entire span than during his long-awaited assassination of his own. The fact that it makes him one of the quintessential of The Absurd Man. There's nothing much more haunting to imagine a man laugh and be happy about his death minutes.<br><br><i>"Men are never convinced of your reasons, of your sincerity, of the seriousness of your sufferings, except by your death." - Albert Camus, The Fall </i><br><br>It's interesting to experience the same absurdity from different points of view referring to the novel The Stranger and the Essay The Myth of Sisyphus and reading those prior to this play, it ultimately devours the paranoia and perils of this meaningless philosophy as the very existence is.<br><br>Camus was one of the very few writers in the history, at least in my view, who didn't say 'I'm wise, I'm wise, I'm wise' in every page of their books. Like Caligula said, a great book is a great evil. This one sure is. <br><br><br>Some of my favorite Caligula's quote by Camus<br><br><i><br>"I don't like writers. I can't bear the way they lie. Their irritating delight in their own profundity. If they ever actually listened to themselves how insignificant the were and they'd choke on their own wit. Go on, get out, you make me sick."<br><br>"Look, Caesonia. Nothing makes sense anymore. Honesty, dignity - what can you say about them? The wisdom of the ages: nothing means anything anymore. Everything turns to ashes in front of fear. Fear, Caesonia, beautiful and pure emotion - so precious- because it draws its authenticity from the guts."<br><br>"Talking of death, I've had a good idea which I want to share with you all. I think my reign has been too happy. We haven't had a plague, or religious strife, not even a revolution. In short, it doesn't look like we'll go down in history. I thought that was a shame. Do you know what I mean? So I decided that I would be your plague."<br><br>"Other people create art because they have no power. <br>I don't need art. I live it."</i></span>

<div id="review_1186579576" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="SmallToothedSmile" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5400186-smalltoothedsmile"><img alt="SmallToothedSmile" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1430364228p2/5400186.jpg" /></a>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1186579576?book_show_action=true">Jan 31, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="SmallToothedSmile" class="user" itemprop="url" name="SmallToothedSmile" href="/user/show/5400186-smalltoothedsmile">SmallToothedSmile</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1186579576" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11744071745648856679">This is an empathetic representation of the the tyrannical emperor, Caligula. After the death of his sister-lover, Caligula seems to have gone completely mad as he orders death upon many innocents and causes humiliation to his team of particiens. However, I interpret his actions, though vicious and fatal, as artistic expression. Indeed, Caligula identifies with the young poet, Scipio, who he describes as being "as for good as I am for bad". Therefore, Caligula, believing himself an artist, has c</span>
  <span id="freeText11744071745648856679" style="display:none">This is an empathetic representation of the the tyrannical emperor, Caligula. After the death of his sister-lover, Caligula seems to have gone completely mad as he orders death upon many innocents and causes humiliation to his team of particiens. However, I interpret his actions, though vicious and fatal, as artistic expression. Indeed, Caligula identifies with the young poet, Scipio, who he describes as being "as for good as I am for bad". Therefore, Caligula, believing himself an artist, has chosen life on earth, and death for that matter, to be his oeuvre.<br /><br /><br />Interestingly, I read that Camus had originally intended to play the role of Caligula himself. That being, I wonder if there is some sort of semi-auto-biographical tendency of this work.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11744071745648856679" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <td colspan="2">

<span id="freeTextContainer2286986770895813968">Caligula / Albert Camus / Translated by Stuart Gilbert / Read on December 28, 2018/ My Score from 5: 2<br/>A play about a king who went crazy after his sister passed away and started murdering people and even his own men. Honestly, I didn’t like it and found it absurd and kind of boring and pointless. There were some interesting conversations among characters which I mention some of them:<br/>“How hard, how cruel it is, this process of becoming a man!”<br/>“A man can’t live without some reason for living.”<br/>“Bey</span>
  <span id="freeText2286986770895813968" style="display:none">Caligula / Albert Camus / Translated by Stuart Gilbert / Read on December 28, 2018/ My Score from 5: 2<br />A play about a king who went crazy after his sister passed away and started murdering people and even his own men. Honestly, I didn’t like it and found it absurd and kind of boring and pointless. There were some interesting conversations among characters which I mention some of them:<br />“How hard, how cruel it is, this process of becoming a man!”<br />“A man can’t live without some reason for living.”<br />“Beyond the frontier of pain lies a splendid, sterile happiness.”<br />“Men weep because…the world’s all wrong.”<br />To sum up, it was dark in an absurd way. It didn’t make any sense and led to no reasonable or meaningful result. This is the first work of Camus that I didn’t like that much.<br /></span>

  <span id="freeText11287613579169250157" style="display:none">4.5<br />all these executions have an equal importance-from which it follows that none has any.<br /><br />This world has no importance; once a man realizes that, he wins his freedom. And that is why I hate you, you and your kind; because you are not free.<br /><br />I want to drown the sky in the sea, to infuse ugliness with beauty, to wring a laugh from pain. <br /><br />And living, my dear, is the opposite of loving. <br /><br />All true passion has a spice of cruelty. <br /><br />There's nothing like hatred for developing the intelligence. <br /><br />All I know is that everything I feel or think of turns to love.<br /><br />Loneliness! What do you know of it? Only the loneliness of poets and weaklings. You prate of loneliness, but you don’t realise that one is never alone. Always we are attended by the same load of the future and the past. Those we have killed are always with us. But they are no great trouble. It’s those we have loved, those who loved us and whom we did not love; regrets, desires, bitterness and sweetness, whores and gods, the gang celestial! Always, always with us! <br /><br />I've merely realized that there's only one way of getting even with the gods. All that's needed is to be as cruel as they. <br /><br />If you did, you'd know that any man can play lead in the divine comedy and become a god. All he needs is to harden his heart. <br /><br />All I want, Helicon, is the moon. For the rest, I've always known what will kill me. I haven't yet exhausted all that is to keep me living. That's why I want the moon.<br /><br />Drusilla old would have been far worse than Drusilla dead.<br /><br />I live, I kill, I exercise the rapturous power of a destroyer, compared with which the power of a creator is merest child's play.</span>

<div id="review_1972236744" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1972236744" />
    <a title="Claudio" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18202178-claudio"><img alt="Claudio" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605140385p2/18202178.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1972236744?book_show_action=true">Sep 29, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Claudio" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Claudio" href="/user/show/18202178-claudio">Claudio</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/19445684-caligola">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18202178-claudio?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18202178-claudio?shelf=theatre">theatre</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1972236744" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5944977276442610259">Short and intense.<br/><br/>Caligula is a distillation of evil in so many ways. And yet, I'm willing to bet that any reader would recognize, deep down, a part of them that refuses to condemn or reject this sadistic tyrant/crazed maniac, and that ends up secretely enjoying his ruthless cruelty.<br/><br/>The more we rationalize and push Caligula's absurdity out of sight, the more we are silently (and weirdly) drawn to him and to his tragic state of mind, detached from reality and increasingly torn apart by power and </span>
  <span id="freeText5944977276442610259" style="display:none">Short and intense.<br /><br />Caligula is a distillation of evil in so many ways. And yet, I'm willing to bet that any reader would recognize, deep down, a part of them that refuses to condemn or reject this sadistic tyrant/crazed maniac, and that ends up secretely enjoying his ruthless cruelty.<br /><br />The more we rationalize and push Caligula's absurdity out of sight, the more we are silently (and weirdly) drawn to him and to his tragic state of mind, detached from reality and increasingly torn apart by power and freedom. In a sense, Caligula reminded me of Nietzsche's Child, the innocent creator, third and final stage on the way to becoming a <i>übermensch</i>. Albeit, of course, in much darker shades.<br /><br />This is why Camus hits us so hard: by portraying this ravaged and childlike Emperor-demon, he is forcing us to face our own share of absurdity, up to the point where we feel ashamed and disoriented. At least, that's what it did to me. Maybe I should be concerned.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5944977276442610259" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer17926096194120879565">Very interesting...I found the themes of authority and blind faith to be quite engaging, perhaps a glimpse into the lives of the average citizen of just about any country. The discussions based on freedom and justice were quite interesting, too. In true Camus fashion, the absurd is omnipresent in just about everything Caligula says in this play, so it was kind of fun to pick out the absurdities and see how they related to what was going on in the story. I'm not going to lie - I thought this play</span>
  <span id="freeText17926096194120879565" style="display:none">Very interesting...I found the themes of authority and blind faith to be quite engaging, perhaps a glimpse into the lives of the average citizen of just about any country. The discussions based on freedom and justice were quite interesting, too. In true Camus fashion, the absurd is omnipresent in just about everything Caligula says in this play, so it was kind of fun to pick out the absurdities and see how they related to what was going on in the story. I'm not going to lie - I thought this play would be wrought with scandal and filth, so I was slightly disappointed by how few the references to the historical Caligula's sexual appetites were...hélas! But still an interesting story nonetheless. I wish I could see it performed somewhere...in French...<br /><br />If you read this play in the near-future, I'd love to hear your thoughts on the masculine/feminine interchange, specifically the fact that Caligula refers to the men in his senate in the feminine (ma chérie, as opposed to mon cher) and that his parade through town displays him dressed as the goddess Venus (goddess of love, but also a very cruel personage, much like our Caligula).<br /><br />Anything else you'd like to discuss? I'm all ears!</span>

<div id="review_225850051" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/225850051" />
    <a title="Elliott Cross" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6667246-elliott-cross"><img alt="Elliott Cross" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442612285p2/6667246.jpg" /></a>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/225850051?book_show_action=true">Oct 20, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elliott Cross" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elliott Cross" href="/user/show/6667246-elliott-cross">Elliott Cross</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer225850051&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer225850051" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1640299010854672202">I first read Camus’ play Caligula when at university in 2002. It struck a cord with me then and I welcomed the chance to read the play again. After the death of his beloved sister, Caligula sees life as empty and meaningless and love and honour as mere vanity. He therefore sets himself on a path to suppress all human feeling and decency and to push the very boundaries of the possible to attain ‘the moon’. He wields unlimited power and kills without mercy or pity, claiming, in his own particular </span>
  <span id="freeText1640299010854672202" style="display:none">I first read Camus’ play Caligula when at university in 2002. It struck a cord with me then and I welcomed the chance to read the play again. After the death of his beloved sister, Caligula sees life as empty and meaningless and love and honour as mere vanity. He therefore sets himself on a path to suppress all human feeling and decency and to push the very boundaries of the possible to attain ‘the moon’. He wields unlimited power and kills without mercy or pity, claiming, in his own particular vision, that it’s all ‘part of the plan’. He taunts those around him, until they are forced to obey his last wish, which is to kill him. Caligula believes that he – as the most powerful autocrat in the world – can achieve the impossible by overcoming and transcending all that is possible and reach a sort of divine understanding of life. He sacrifices everything – love, morality, honour, decency, sanity – but, in the end, he is alone and none the wiser, and his great mission ends in futility and violent death. Camus brilliantly conveys the spiritual turmoil that humans go through as they try to make sense of their place in the world. His characters are full of warmth and depth, cruelty and wrath, and are torn apart by the painful contradictions of life.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1640299010854672202" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/740699994?book_show_action=true">Oct 13, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Scarlett" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Scarlett" href="/user/show/1970438-scarlett">Scarlett</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title=" 9788420636962" href="/book/show/3103010-cal-gula">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1970438-scarlett?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1970438-scarlett?shelf=theater">theater</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer740699994" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5480140306164695747">This was a rather harmless and forgettable play by an author that has otherwise greatly impressed me. However, there is a certain purity to Caligula's madness and violence, and that kept me interested enough in reaching the end, which thanks to its brevity, arrived some 45 minutes after I had started it.<br/><br/>Caligula wants to achieve immortality, to be god-like, to be completely free. That is his pretense and the way he justifies his acts of violence as a Roman emperor. He seemed to have been a good </span>
  <span id="freeText5480140306164695747" style="display:none">This was a rather harmless and forgettable play by an author that has otherwise greatly impressed me. However, there is a certain purity to Caligula's madness and violence, and that kept me interested enough in reaching the end, which thanks to its brevity, arrived some 45 minutes after I had started it.<br /><br />Caligula wants to achieve immortality, to be god-like, to be completely free. That is his pretense and the way he justifies his acts of violence as a Roman emperor. He seemed to have been a good and fair ruler before the death of his sister/lover Drusilla, so naturally one wonders whether the loss of love can lead a man to great acts of insanity, and if you take a look at life, doesn't this happen every other day? Nevertheless, it is not clear whether these acts are a consequence of her death or of something else; this, perhaps, renders the play unrealistic and detracts from its greatness.<br /><br />Side note: "Caligula" means "little soldier's boot", the diminutive form of "caliga", a hob-nailed military boot. He earned this nickname from accompanying his father during his campaigns in Germania.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5480140306164695747" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ali" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ali" href="/user/show/4693-ali">Ali</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer325486" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10322192738899232361">A man at the top of power, able to do what ever he wants, searching for impossible to change the world, and all of a sudden, when his sister (also his beloved) dies, Caligula encourage the death to take him away, as he believes that his freedom was not a true one. Maybe my interest in Caligula is because I've seen a rather nice performance of the play ...<br/><br/>این نمایش نامه ی کامو یک بار توسط ابوالحسن نجفی به فارسی ترجمه شده و نتشارات نیل آن را منتشر کرده (1346) و بار دیگر با ترجمه ی شورانگیز فرخ توس</span>
  <span id="freeText10322192738899232361" style="display:none">A man at the top of power, able to do what ever he wants, searching for impossible to change the world, and all of a sudden, when his sister (also his beloved) dies, Caligula encourage the death to take him away, as he believes that his freedom was not a true one. Maybe my interest in Caligula is because I've seen a rather nice performance of the play ...<br /><br />این نمایش نامه ی کامو یک بار توسط ابوالحسن نجفی به فارسی ترجمه شده و نتشارات نیل آن را منتشر کرده (1346) و بار دیگر با ترجمه ی شورانگیز فرخ توسط انتشارات مروارید منتشر شده (1347) است. اواخر دهه ی چهل یا اوایل دهه ی پنجاه شمسی "آربی آوانسیان" این نمایش نامه را با اجرایی تماشایی در تیاتر شهر به صحنه برد.<br /></span>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1761303321" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3987952302298857724">An extraordinary play by the renowned writer. Camus speculates on a new and radically different reason for Caligula's actions that have little to do with madness and more with following a logic that on the surface appears acceptable but when is applied properly and followed through to the ultimate conclusion turns out to be monstrous. The dialogue is philosophically incisive and the action moves remorselessly towards a spectacular denouement.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('eEneR7DkQ8HnyofH+tSEBYnWzJtc3soVik1RXybaHykTy9NbyQ8brLny0hOhdR/enU/7hyujXvCKLaoPhJ8ZrA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('CaJMQkgtoA6jtZCC53EWGU/KLbCRIloeq7x+VA4YU+hiIEFeMcb4Y/2NxVa80I3CW1MarOZfzvur3IUErF1VbQ==')})
  }

Eh bien, reste pur ! A quoi cela servira-t-il et pourquoi viens-tu parmi nous ? La pureté, c\'est une idée de fakir et de moine. Vous autres, les intellectuels, les anarchistes bourgeois, vous en tirez prétexte pour ne rien faire. Ne rien faire, rester immobile, serrer les coudes contre le corps, porter des gants. Moi j\'ai les mains sales. Jusqu\'aux coudes. Je la ai plongées dans la merde et dans le sang. »\nCréée en 1948 au théâtre Antoine par François Périer et André Luguet, Les Mains sales, pièce sur l\'engagement politique, est une des œuvres théâtrales les plus retentissantes de l\'auteur de Huis clos, des Mouches, de La Putain respectueuse, des Séquestrés d\'Altona, du Diable et le bon Dieu.<\/span>\n  <a       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/957894.Albert_Camus">About Albert Camus</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/957894.Albert_Camus"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1506091612p3/957894.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/957894.Albert_Camus">Albert Camus
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
21,777
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer13243970894461712609"><b>Albert Camus</b> (1913-1960) was a representative of non-metropolitan French literature. His origin in Algeria and his experiences there in the thirties were dominating influences in his thought and work. Of semi-proletarian parents, early attached to intellectual circles of strongly revolutionary tendencies, with a deep interest in philosophy (only chance prevented him from pursuing a university care</span>
  <span id="freeText13243970894461712609" style="display:none"><b>Albert Camus</b> (1913-1960) was a representative of non-metropolitan French literature. His origin in Algeria and his experiences there in the thirties were dominating influences in his thought and work. Of semi-proletarian parents, early attached to intellectual circles of strongly revolutionary tendencies, with a deep interest in philosophy (only chance prevented him from pursuing a university career in that field), he came to France at the age of twenty-five. The man and the times met: Camus joined the resistance movement during the occupation and after the liberation was a columnist for the newspaper Combat. But his journalistic activities had been chiefly a response to the demands of the time; in 1947 Camus retired from political journalism and, besides writing his fiction and essays, was very active in the theatre as producer and playwright (e.g., Caligula, 1944). He also adapted plays by Calderon, Lope de Vega, Dino Buzzati, and Faulkner's Requiem for a Nun. His love for the theatre may be traced back to his membership in L'Equipe, an Algerian theatre group, whose "collective creation" Révolte dans les Asturies (1934) was banned for political reasons.<br /><br />The essay Le Mythe de Sisyphe (The Myth of Sisyphus), 1942, expounds Camus's notion of the absurd and of its acceptance with "the total absence of hope, which has nothing to do with despair, a continual refusal, which must not be confused with renouncement - and a conscious dissatisfaction". Meursault, central character of L'Étranger (The Stranger), 1942, illustrates much of this essay: man as the nauseated victim of the absurd orthodoxy of habit, later - when the young killer faces execution - tempted by despair, hope, and salvation. Dr. Rieux of La Peste (The Plague), 1947, who tirelessly attends the plague-stricken citizens of Oran, enacts the revolt against a world of the absurd and of injustice, and confirms Camus's words: "We refuse to despair of mankind. Without having the unreasonable ambition to save men, we still want to serve them". Other well-known works of Camus are La Chute (The Fall), 1956, and L'Exil et le royaume (Exile and the Kingdom), 1957. His austere search for moral order found its aesthetic correlative in the classicism of his art. He was a stylist of great purity and intense concentration and rationality.<br /><br />Chinese <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/14785167.______" title="阿尔贝·加缪" rel="nofollow noopener">阿尔贝·加缪</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="13243970894461712609" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
